<div id="review_655920615" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/655920615" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/655920615?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9780385367431" href="/book/show/18664879-the-lowland">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=global-intrigue">global-intrigue</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=asia">asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=mesmerizing">mesmerizing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=war-stories">war-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer655920615" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3858777157588304721">A melancholic tale narrated with restraint and distance. Seeing how the tone was set in the beginning, I didn't expect to be drawn in to the story, wasn't sure what to expect.<br/><br/>But call her (Lahiri) the plot whisperer.<br/><br/>Here, time will fascinate you, moving abruptly, standing still, spanning generations and decades--yet still managing to stay organic to the plot. The plot and story have so many intricacies that it is time which directs it all. <br/><br/>Picture two young boys growing up in the 1960s amid the </span>
  <span id="freeText3858777157588304721" style="display:none">A melancholic tale narrated with restraint and distance. Seeing how the tone was set in the beginning, I didn't expect to be drawn in to the story, wasn't sure what to expect.<br /><br />But call her (Lahiri) the plot whisperer.<br /><br />Here, time will fascinate you, moving abruptly, standing still, spanning generations and decades--yet still managing to stay organic to the plot. The plot and story have so many intricacies that it is time which directs it all. <br /><br />Picture two young boys growing up in the 1960s amid the lowland of Calcutta, getting into mischief that suits boys their age. In the background, picture the remnants of the partition of India which led to the division of the Bengal province; picture the infamous <i>Naxalite </i>movement in India (communism; Maoist ideology, Marxist-Leninst politics). Picture one brother being headstrong and the other more conscientious. Picture yourself embracing their closeness at the beginning while also being uncomfortably aware of subtle hints from the third-person narrator that something is about to put a wedge between this brotherhood. <br /><br />Now fast forward. <br /><br />See them grow older; meet a bookish young wife who, even in the midst of conservative society, has feminist ideals and crude ambition. <br />See an obstinate and cruel mother-in-law. <br />See a marriage made in love and another conceived for convenience. <br />See the wetlands of a Calcutta neighborhood contrasted with the coast of Rhode Island. <br />See intellectualism battle civil unrest. <br />See the living suffer, watch the dead live.<br />See the ugliness of post traumatic stress disorder. <br />See one mother live with anguish, see another live with ambivalence.<br />See your skin crawl from a mother's abandonment.<br />See what it means to live a life of exile. <br />See what happens when people refuse to reconcile the past.<br />See the ghost who ruins.<br />See happiness intertwined with misery and agony. <br />See how people can be raised the same, yet lead different lives. <br /><br />You read some books and through gestures, symbolism and impeccable details, they subtlety teach you things you never knew. This is one such book. Though stylistically different, parts of it reminded me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7274337.The_Invisible_Bridge" title="The Invisible Bridge by Julie Orringer" rel="nofollow noopener">The Invisible Bridge</a><br /><br />This novel has the sophistication of a political novel. I hate to even do this comparison, but close your eyes, and you would think that it was a male author writing about male characters. Told from the third-person narrator point of view, the tone embodies the coping mechanism of the characters: stoicism--just what is needed to accompany a heartrending story without the melodramatics. At times the narration is so distant, it is as if only the storyteller is present (which, by the way, the storytelling here is stupendous). Then just at the right moment, the narration gets closer, so close that you and the character become one, you see their point of view clearly and you may not agree with them but you certainly 'get' them. <br /><br />I love when I sense characters, when the plot doesn't stray but goes forward with such purpose that all I can do is follow to hear what happens next, learn what I'm supposed to learn. Love when I start to feel sorry for characters, wondering why they all end up so miserable, and then I learn that...wait...I'm wrong because in the end, they all don't end up in misery--though for some it is a long time coming. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3858777157588304721" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_700269776" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/700269776" />
    <a title="Antonomasia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia"><img alt="Antonomasia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605185148p2/6249204.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/700269776?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antonomasia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antonomasia" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia">Antonomasia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781408828113" href="/book/show/18269724-the-lowland">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Antonomasia by: </span>
      Booker longlist
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=modern-american-fiction">modern-american-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=booker">booker</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=decade-2010s">decade-2010s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=indian-subcontinent">indian-subcontinent</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer700269776" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5586415303740470039">The political history was interesting - 1960s student radicals in India - but most of <i>The Lowland</i>, which takes place in subsequent decades, is just another overly serious modern American family saga (immigrant subtype). <br/><br/>The unquestioned contrast in personalities of the two central brothers has a mythological quality but Lahiri's writing never achieves the grandeur befitting that. Sensible Subhash would, I'm sure, make an excellent, nice and reliable work colleague but written about as he was her</span>
  <span id="freeText5586415303740470039" style="display:none">The political history was interesting - 1960s student radicals in India - but most of <i>The Lowland</i>, which takes place in subsequent decades, is just another overly serious modern American family saga (immigrant subtype). <br /><br />The unquestioned contrast in personalities of the two central brothers has a mythological quality but Lahiri's writing never achieves the grandeur befitting that. Sensible Subhash would, I'm sure, make an excellent, nice and reliable work colleague but written about as he was here he was of little interest. There wasn't enough insight into fiery commie Udayin, or enough story told from his viewpoint - there is a certain amount of judgement in this book that moderation and domesticity are best. I quite liked Gauri and Bela between approx. pp.200-300, but still, all the US storylines were things I've heard many times before and which don't much interest me as a subject for fiction. ("Stuff normal people like" was a phrase that kept popping into my head to characterise this novel and Lahiri, generally. Alongside Norah Jones, James Blunt and that ilk. Later I cheered <a href="http://www.spectator.co.uk/books/8998261/philip-hensher-reviews-the-man-booker-prize-longlist/" rel="nofollow noopener">Philip Hensher</a>'s snark about the book's <i>American-airport-bestseller-style</i>.)<br /><br />The most laughable episode was this:<br />(From memory as I don't have a copy with me)<br />"Do you like it here?" she asked him.<br />No-one had asked him this before... [He has been there years and this conversation obviously represents his stoicism and unselfishness, and that others have been taking him for granted. <br />Two or three paragraphs describe a river and a bridge and him looking at same, before he actually <i>replies</i> in any shape or form.]<br />What an exhausted old nag of a trope. I couldn't quite believe that readers of a highly-lauded author are still supposed to fall for this rubbish.<br /><br />The worst thing of all? Lahiri's writing style. Her aversion to the word "and"; dull synonyms instead of adjectives. Minimal description, underelaboration leaving me too often unmoved and uncaring about the characters. Oh, the drear! All the bloody commas! For days after I finished <i>The Lowland</i> I cringed when I saw certain Lahiri-like sentence structures in other books. <br />It's a cumulative effect which is unlikely to bother anyone on the basis of a few quotations. She's at it here, too, in <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2013/08/25/t-magazine/25writers-rooms.html?_r=2&amp;" rel="nofollow noopener">a twee NYT article</a> I clicked on for some reason. <i>...when I’d only read and heard about Italy, before I’d ever come to Rome</i> is an example of the type of sentence I grew to loathe over 340 pages - almost every one featuring this sort of phrasing - and which now makes me want to throw something at the screen. Her few paragraphs in the article take something beautiful and render it mundane and lifeless to me. <br /><br />From what I'd seen of previews, reader-reviews, and snideness such as <a href="http://www.lrb.co.uk/v32/n18/elif-batuman/get-a-real-degree" rel="nofollow noopener">this in the LRB</a>: <i>Even within the seemingly homogeneous sphere of the university English department, a schism has opened up between literary scholarship and creative writing: disciplines which differ in their points of reference (Samuel Richardson v. Jhumpa Lahiri)...</i><br />I had suspected I would not enjoy Lahiri's writing, but not that I would almost hate it.<br /><br />For perhaps the first half of the book I considered myself an ungrateful wretch, having got my mitts on a copy three weeks prior to official release whilst so many people who'd love to read it hadn't. When I started to dread picking it up, and even more dread those sentences, I must admit this guilt quite subsided.<br /><br />3 stars is generous considering how much <i>The Lowland</i> grated, but as I was setting it alongside other Booker longlisted titles I had to concede that it is a perfectly competent novel that sits together well and makes sense. However, writing this review is making me reconsider whether I should rate this dull humourless litfic over more populist sort of novels which were stereotyped or had plot mis-steps. They were at least a bit more enjoyable to read.<br /><br />ETA, Nov. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/99438733" rel="nofollow noopener">This review</a> of <i>Unaccustomed Earth</i> eloquently says a number of things I wish I'd said about Lahiri and her writing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5586415303740470039" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_748431989" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/748431989" />
    <a title="Julie Ehlers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2120450-julie-ehlers"><img alt="Julie Ehlers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446217915p2/2120450.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/748431989?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Ehlers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Ehlers" href="/user/show/2120450-julie-ehlers">Julie Ehlers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2120450-julie-ehlers?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer748431989" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11547337885475952651">I see Jhumpa Lahiri as kind of an old-fashioned novelist in the sense that she genuinely cares about things like character development, setting, and storytelling. She's not trying to impress you with how clever she is; she's not setting up some "twist" that's going to make you rethink everything that came before it. She's all about providing an immersive experience that earns all of its emotional high points and epiphanies. This is all to the good, in my opinion. I wish there were more writers o</span>
  <span id="freeText11547337885475952651" style="display:none">I see Jhumpa Lahiri as kind of an old-fashioned novelist in the sense that she genuinely cares about things like character development, setting, and storytelling. She's not trying to impress you with how clever she is; she's not setting up some "twist" that's going to make you rethink everything that came before it. She's all about providing an immersive experience that earns all of its emotional high points and epiphanies. This is all to the good, in my opinion. I wish there were more writers out there who took their responsibility to their story as seriously as Lahiri does.<br /><br />As for <i>The Lowland</i> specifically, I found its plotline much more intriguing than that of <i>The Namesake</i>, but it was somewhat less emotionally satisfying for me. Upon reflection, I think this is simply because <i>The Namesake</i> stayed in Gogol's head nearly the entire time, whereas <i>The Lowland</i> skipped around among Subhash, Gauri, and, to a lesser extent, a few other characters. After <i>The Namesake</i> I longed for a Lahiri novel from a female viewpoint, but sadly here I found the Gauri sections somewhat unsatisfying and mostly couldn't wait to get back to Subhash, the heart of the book. Still, I thought <i>The Lowland</i> was excellent overall and would definitely recommend it to fans of her other works, or fans of good fiction in general.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11547337885475952651" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_542349668" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/542349668" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2228181-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507660688p2/2228181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/542349668?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/2228181-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer542349668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13709365557755846472">First let me say that Jhumpa Lahiri is my goddess of literature. I read a lot – maybe 75 books a year – and I have rarely fallen under the spell of a book the way I did with Interpreter of Maladies. Her follow-up collection of short stories, Unaccustomed Earth, was also an unqualified 5-star success.<br/><br/>So I was dying to get my hands on her new novel, The Lowland. I read through it eagerly but I closed the last page with mixed feelings.<br/><br/>Let’s start with the good: Ms. Lahiri is a natural-born storytel</span>
  <span id="freeText13709365557755846472" style="display:none">First let me say that Jhumpa Lahiri is my goddess of literature. I read a lot – maybe 75 books a year – and I have rarely fallen under the spell of a book the way I did with Interpreter of Maladies. Her follow-up collection of short stories, Unaccustomed Earth, was also an unqualified 5-star success.<br /><br />So I was dying to get my hands on her new novel, The Lowland. I read through it eagerly but I closed the last page with mixed feelings.<br /><br />Let’s start with the good: Ms. Lahiri is a natural-born storyteller. In this book, she introduces two brothers, close in age who are poles apart – Udayan, the revolutionary brother who gets caught up in the Mao-inspired Naxalite movement to wipe out poverty in India and his more reserved and dutiful brother, Subhash, who leaves home to pursue an academic and scientific life in Rhode Island. When Udayan inevitably gets swept into a revolutionary movement that turns out badly, Subhash returns home –briefly – and picks up the pieces, including an attempt to heal the emotional scars of his brother’s young wife.<br /><br />As the plot goes on – and it is not my desire to encapsulate the plot or to create spoilers – about 70 years of family history is condensed into a mere 340 pages. Themes play out and then they play out again: the connections that make and break us, the intertwining to people we cannot truly see or know, the way we are defined by the place we call “home”, the quiet differences we make in the world. It’s all wound up in the history of India and indeed, Ms. Lahiri is at her very finest when she’s describing Indian customs or lifestyles as only an insider can.<br /><br />There’s some lovely craftsmanship here, not bells and whistles, but quiet and contemplative -- even shimmering – moments. The problem is, I never found it to be very compelling. Because of all the years and generations (four of them) that Ms. Lahiri has to cover, she can only provide sketches of her characters. And they never truly come alive.<br /><br />Yes, Udayan is the fiery revolutionary…but what made him so and why was he willing to sacrifice so much (the headiness of youth and a sense of fairness should only be the beginning). His wife, Guari, who eventually bonds with Subhash, was an enigma to be throughout. She is a distanced character, and her actions begin to feel somewhat predictable; the reader is never treated to her resonance and depth. And Bela, her daughter, is only revealed in limited emotional scope.<br /><br />A novel, unlike a short story, demands a considerable emotional tension, a multi-textured richness that makes characters leap off the page. I never really sensed the two-dimensionality, possibly because the story line was multi-generational and ambitious. The litmus test of whether or not you will love this book is this: if you loved Namesake, this is definitely a book for you, since stylistically, there are similarities. If you are, instead, a fan of her short stories, you may or may not be engaged. Judge for yourself.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13709365557755846472" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_514805147" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/514805147" />
    <a title="Jessica Jeffers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/188447-jessica-jeffers"><img alt="Jessica Jeffers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459125628p2/188447.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/514805147?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica Jeffers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica Jeffers" href="/user/show/188447-jessica-jeffers">Jessica Jeffers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/188447-jessica-jeffers?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/188447-jessica-jeffers?shelf=authors-of-color">authors-of-color</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer514805147" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7409730757653678370">Jhumpa Lahiri is, hands-down, my favorite writer. <i>The Namesake</i> is part of the reason why I chose to pursue a career in books, and it was very nearly the subject of my master's thesis. I'm not much of a short story reader, but <i>Unaccustomed Earth</i> made me wish I could be a writer. <br/><br/>I just love the way that she examines human nature. Sometimes her themes can be a little repetitive, but her insights are so sharp. I love how her work tends to emphasizes the smaller moments of her characters' lives -- <i>T</i></span>
  <span id="freeText7409730757653678370" style="display:none">Jhumpa Lahiri is, hands-down, my favorite writer. <i>The Namesake</i> is part of the reason why I chose to pursue a career in books, and it was very nearly the subject of my master's thesis. I'm not much of a short story reader, but <i>Unaccustomed Earth</i> made me wish I could be a writer. <br /><br />I just love the way that she examines human nature. Sometimes her themes can be a little repetitive, but her insights are so sharp. I love how her work tends to emphasizes the smaller moments of her characters' lives -- <i>The Namesake</i> condenses an entire thirty-something years into less than 300 pages, but never felt like it was lacking in depth. <br /><br /><i>The Lowland</i> follows a similar structure, but here Lahiri covers more years and more generations in more pages. It doesn't work quite as well as <i>The Namesake</i> did, but it's still quite powerful. <br /><br />The book begins with two brothers, Subhash and Udayan, in 1960s Calcutta. Udayan becomes involved in an underground communist movement, while Subhash pursues postgraduate education in America. When Udayan's politics cost him his life, Subhash returns home and learns that his sister-in-law, Guari, is pregnant. Guari is received coldly by the brothers' parents because the marriage was not an arranged one. Knowing that she will essentially be alone, Subhash decides to marry her and take her back to America with him so that she may have a safer, more economically secure life. The books follows their lives well into the twenty-first century.<br /><br />I think that the metaphorical elements of this book, the aspects that emphasize themes and broader ideas work quite well. That's where Lahiri shines the most. The contrast of Guari's unarranged marriage to Udayan versus a might-as-well-be arranged marriage to Subhash. Guari and Subhash’s differing approaches to parenthood contrasted against their differing paths to parenthood. How their daughter Bela’s approach to life varies from their own. The sacrfices made by each of the brothers in their pursuit of what each sees as the noble path. <br /><br />Unfortunately, the characters in <i>The Lowland</i> don't shine quite as much. Over the course of this book, we see Udayan and Subhash as young children, experiencing things that will lead to Udayan's desire to join the Naxalite movement during his college years. We see Subhash grow into an old man and Guari's daughter grow into middle age. We see the brothers’ parents reach the end of their lives. And yet, I never felt connected to any of these characters quite the way I expected to. They behaved in complex-yet-predictable ways, but their development sometimes felt mechanical in a way that didn’t allow them to rise up off the page. <br /><br />Lahiri is a brilliant writer and I can’t not recommend this book. Start with her other works first, if you haven’t already. Read this one for the thematic exploration of cultural and familal obligations – if you want a story where the characters grab you, you may want to pass over this one. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7409730757653678370" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_516112249" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/516112249" />
    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3642045-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/516112249?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue" href="/user/show/3642045-sue">Sue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/17854650-the-lowland">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer516112249" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8232439934281600027">In Calcutta in the 1960s two brothers come of age amid a confusing cluster of economic, social, religious, and political changes that were to disrupt India for years. Subhash and his younger brother Udayan were inseparable throughout their youth. With strict parents and expectations for their lives, they began to bridle as they approached college age. Both were bright boys, both with plans for their futures but here their lives begin to separate and their lives and family to splinter. <br/><br/>Subhash is</span>
  <span id="freeText8232439934281600027" style="display:none">In Calcutta in the 1960s two brothers come of age amid a confusing cluster of economic, social, religious, and political changes that were to disrupt India for years. Subhash and his younger brother Udayan were inseparable throughout their youth. With strict parents and expectations for their lives, they began to bridle as they approached college age. Both were bright boys, both with plans for their futures but here their lives begin to separate and their lives and family to splinter. <br /><br />Subhash is the student, applies himself and ultimately decides to attend graduate school on another lowland, the coastal northeast of the United States. Udayan, while also an excellent student, becomes involved in the Naxilite movement and dedicates himself to the Indian poor. He learns Marxist theory and the teachings of Mao. <br /><br />The parents assume that their sons will ultimately return to the home of their childhood, the home by the lowland, that they will bring brides to this home, women who will then run the home, bear children and carry on traditions. But Lahiri---and Subhash and Udayan---have a different story to tell, one of tangled relationships, lost chances, families broken and rebuilt, love lost and found. And Lahiri does this with her signature beautiful prose which I have experienced previously through her short stories.<br /><br />The character development throughout this novel was done in a manner that drew me in and along. Interestingly, the only time I found myself losing my affection for the novel was one section dedicated to an unsympathetic character. I realized a short time later that it was the character's thought processes and decisions that bothered me, not the writing. <br /><br />I have found this a difficult book to review because there is so much in it and it is difficult to avoid important plot points. I did learn much about Indian history of the 1960s, particularly the Naxilite movement. I do recommend this book and very likely will read it again (and may raise the rating).</span>
  <a data-text-id="8232439934281600027" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2717865233" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2717865233" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4643325-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535048395p2/4643325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2717865233?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4643325-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=readingwomenchallenge2019">readingwomenchallenge2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2717865233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1168190078059188079">I have had mixed feelings about Lahiri in the past, but I enjoyed this novel whilst being unsure of what Lahiri was trying to do with it. There isn’t a cast of thousands, but it is a family drama starting in India and moving to the US. The focus of the novel is two brothers Subhash and Udayan. They are born in Calcutta fifteen months apart, just after the Second World War. There is a patch of land near where they live in Tollygunge with two ponds and close to the exclusive Tolly club. The brothe</span>
  <span id="freeText1168190078059188079" style="display:none">I have had mixed feelings about Lahiri in the past, but I enjoyed this novel whilst being unsure of what Lahiri was trying to do with it. There isn’t a cast of thousands, but it is a family drama starting in India and moving to the US. The focus of the novel is two brothers Subhash and Udayan. They are born in Calcutta fifteen months apart, just after the Second World War. There is a patch of land near where they live in Tollygunge with two ponds and close to the exclusive Tolly club. The brothers are close and the reader is taken through childhood and into the mid-1960s towards college. Udayan is always the leader. <br />The two brothers begin to drift apart. Subhash goes to college in Rhode Island to study. Udayan becomes involved in radical politics and joins the Naxalite movement, Maoist in politics and potentially violent. Udayan also marries Gauri, in secret, only telling his parents afterwards. Udayan is shot by the police on the patch of ground hear his home. Gauri is pregnant. Subhash returns for the funeral and marries Gauri, although they do not love each other. They return to the US. This all takes place in the first quarter of the book. The rest of the book follows Subhash, Gauri and her daughter Bela up to the present day. <br />Lahiri captures the immigrant experience in America through the eyes of Subhash: <br />“The difference was so extreme that he could not accommodate the two places together in his mind. In this enormous new country, there seemed to be nowhere for the old to reside. There was nothing to link them; he was the sole link.”<br />The first section of the novel is gripping, however the rest is much more evenly paced and is really about living life and dealing with the past. There is a focus on family relationships. There is a sense of destinies displaced and the grinding mundaneness of life. There is a reality to this rather than elegance. And a story of fraternal bonds that linger beyond death. There are niggles, the relationship between Gauri and Subhash, for me didn’t ring true in the way it played out, especially in relation to Bela. The novel does play out the universal nature of human hopes and fears, it is well written and I enjoyed it. But it didn’t really do any more than that. I’m not sure what more I wanted, more of an edge perhaps.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1168190078059188079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2300202634" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2300202634" />
    <a title="Tanmay Jadhav" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65664812-tanmay-jadhav"><img alt="Tanmay Jadhav" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1606054256p2/65664812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2300202634?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanmay Jadhav" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanmay Jadhav" href="/user/show/65664812-tanmay-jadhav">Tanmay Jadhav</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2300202634" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1177989876234047815">Every time i pick up a Jhumpa Lahiri, i feel like i know what i'm getting into and yet every time i find myself proven wrong. <br/><br/>I can ramble for hours about the story and nuances but to be honest the story isn't what makes the book special<br/><br/>Jhumpa's flowing texts with apparently irrelevant descriptions that confluence perfectly with the scenario or as a buildup is what makes her an irreplaceable part of literary history.<br/><br/>The way she can take you between generations and second person perspectives of a</span>
  <span id="freeText1177989876234047815" style="display:none">Every time i pick up a Jhumpa Lahiri, i feel like i know what i'm getting into and yet every time i find myself proven wrong. <br /><br />I can ramble for hours about the story and nuances but to be honest the story isn't what makes the book special<br /><br />Jhumpa's flowing texts with apparently irrelevant descriptions that confluence perfectly with the scenario or as a buildup is what makes her an irreplaceable part of literary history.<br /><br />The way she can take you between generations and second person perspectives of all the characters involved is magical. And the way she puts you right in the middle of the story makes me cry quite often along with the characters. <br /><br />Jhumpa in my opinion paints human emotions like no one i've encountered yet. The way she develops and maintains undertones and core character themes makes you hate her and love her at the same time. <br /><br />I'm terribly sorry for turning this review into a fanboy rant. This book is everything you could ask for after "Namesake"<br /><br />It takes up so many backgrounds. From the Naxal uprising to the bonds of brothers to a compromise marriage that never takes course to the characters, old with regret, trying to set things right or at least get some closure. <br /><br />This is a heart wrenching tale of normal people who find themselves in extraordinary circumstances trying to live normal lives but failing at it. This ability of Jhumpa to create something extraordinary from the lives of normal people is my favorite thing about her. <br /><br />Please read this.<br /><br />Cheers,<br />Tanmay J<br /><br />P.s. 4 stars because i feel like the author had lost her touch in the middle chapters and wasted it on story lines that did not matter much. The book was flawless otherwise.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11379684880408349872">I won't recount the storyline as it's amply available. <br/><br/>Very satisfying read. A must-read! After finishing this book (an ARC from an angel and subsequently paid forward), I had to take a day or so off from reading anything else in order to continue to allow the afterglow of The Lowland to linger.<br/><br/>I found Lahiri's writing to be so precise and her tone so serene. And yet it's taut--that skin on the drum secured very tightly and thus enabling the biggest impact/resistance. The tension rests just bene</span>
  <span id="freeText11379684880408349872" style="display:none">I won't recount the storyline as it's amply available. <br /><br />Very satisfying read. A must-read! After finishing this book (an ARC from an angel and subsequently paid forward), I had to take a day or so off from reading anything else in order to continue to allow the afterglow of The Lowland to linger.<br /><br />I found Lahiri's writing to be so precise and her tone so serene. And yet it's taut--that skin on the drum secured very tightly and thus enabling the biggest impact/resistance. The tension rests just beneath the surface, threatening to break through. <br /><br />The words, tone and pacing have a focused and steady quality that accentuates the emotions, the beauty of the descriptions (e.g., sky, the house, the hairs on his forearms), and the impact of the actions. <br /><br />I was also thrilled by how she crafted the characters. Each character --at each life stage, from childhood on-- rang "true" to me.</span>

<div id="review_726879433" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/726879433" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1826682-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252295186p2/1826682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/726879433?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/1826682-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=parenting">parenting</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=asia">asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=totally-unexpected">totally-unexpected</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer726879433" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15979878299472826377">I am hoping to ‘fillet a stone’ in this review, and separate Lahiri’s writing from her story in this, her latest novel. Lahiri has lavish gifts when it comes to writing. Although <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5439.Interpreter_of_Maladies' title='Interpreter of Maladies by Jhumpa Lahiri' rel='nofollow noopener'>Interpreter of Maladies</a> won so many awards and gave Lahiri encouragement perhaps, I preferred another book of linked stories, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/85301.Unaccustomed_Earth' title='Unaccustomed Earth by Jhumpa Lahiri' rel='nofollow noopener'>Unaccustomed Earth</a>, for its deep insights, faultless language, and for peeling the veil from a culture I can never hope to know intimately.<br/><br/>The writing in this, her latest novel, was, I thought, w</span>
  <span id="freeText15979878299472826377" style="display:none">I am hoping to ‘fillet a stone’ in this review, and separate Lahiri’s writing from her story in this, her latest novel. Lahiri has lavish gifts when it comes to writing. Although <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5439.Interpreter_of_Maladies" title="Interpreter of Maladies by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">Interpreter of Maladies</a> won so many awards and gave Lahiri encouragement perhaps, I preferred another book of linked stories, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/85301.Unaccustomed_Earth" title="Unaccustomed Earth by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">Unaccustomed Earth</a>, for its deep insights, faultless language, and for peeling the veil from a culture I can never hope to know intimately.<br /><br />The writing in this, her latest novel, was, I thought, workmanlike and studious--like her characters in a way, scientists and PhDs, both. And it was too long and wordy, like a dissertation maybe. Regarding the story, for me it held little drama, and seemed a long litany of history for not-so-interesting characters. I did get a sense of Indian culture as scrappy and vibrant at the same time it is smothering and crushing. I do think Lahiri did a good job with the expatriate/immigrant experience—that sense of dislocation and living at a remove. I also thought the inter-national love affair between Holly and Subhash in Rhode Island rang true, neither of the lovers imagining for a moment their families would accept ‘a foreigner’ and they themselves lack the experience and confidence to transgress the life expected of them. And Lahiri gave us some nice images: “[Bela, the baby] breathed with her whole body…like an animal.”<br /><br />Though I am not intimately familiar with Indian lifestyle practices, Lahiri did have me trying to manage feelings of outrage and disbelief about the way Subhash withheld Bela’s parentage from her, despite Bela’s difficult time adjusting when her mother did a runner. It is difficult for me to empathize with an educated man (and woman) of any nationality who watch with equanimity their family disintegrate and do nothing to prevent it, even when the mental health of the child in their care is at stake. I’m sure there are folks out there that act this way, but when Lahiri says Bela came back and <i>thanked</i> her father for not telling her he was her stepfather, I thought perhaps we stepped off the reality train. Maybe it is so—they are Lahiri’s characters after all—but it all felt a little far out there to American me.<br /><br />Another way to look at this book is that it is unadorned, cruel, and often boring--just like life itself. Anita Chaudhuri, an editor at <i>Psychologies</i> magazine, tweeted of the book: "One of the bravest books I've ever read, no tricks, no jokes, just life." I guess that is true, too. It may not be what we want to hear or how we want to live, but there it is, like it or not.<br /><br />This book was shortlisted for the 2013 Man Booker Prize, which was awarded instead to Eleanor Catton’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17333230.The_Luminaries" title="The Luminaries by Eleanor Catton" rel="nofollow noopener">The Luminaries</a>. It is also <a href="”" rel="nofollow noopener">a finalist</a> for 2013 The National Book Award for fiction.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15979878299472826377" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7992906062352820743">As there are two brothers of opposite temperaments in this book, perhaps it is appropriate that I am of two minds about the book. It's above all elegantly written, yet too much is explained, especially in the last three chapters. So, yes, it could've ended three chapters sooner than it did, yet I appreciated why she included them. I questioned the sudden use of no names in the penultimate one, as that seemed to serve no purpose, though its sudden shift to present tense was fine. At times there i</span>
  <span id="freeText7992906062352820743" style="display:none">As there are two brothers of opposite temperaments in this book, perhaps it is appropriate that I am of two minds about the book. It's above all elegantly written, yet too much is explained, especially in the last three chapters. So, yes, it could've ended three chapters sooner than it did, yet I appreciated why she included them. I questioned the sudden use of no names in the penultimate one, as that seemed to serve no purpose, though its sudden shift to present tense was fine. At times there is more telling than showing as years are spooled out, especially for one character, and yet it was done in an almost-forgivable Munro way.<br /><br />If you've read Lahiri before, some of the tone and situations of this novel will seem very familiar at certain points, yet that feeling of familiarity eventually dissipates and then stops altogether. <br /><br />It's a powerful story, and yet with more focus on the actual story, and less perhaps on the themes, I think it could've been even more powerful. I'm a 'character person' for the most part and these are well-drawn, yet I never felt that any of them leaped off the page as by the middle I expected they might.</span>

<div id="review_776658453" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/776658453" />
    <a title="Mal Warwick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4739424-mal-warwick"><img alt="Mal Warwick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294014688p2/4739424.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/776658453?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mal Warwick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mal Warwick" href="/user/show/4739424-mal-warwick">Mal Warwick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739424-mal-warwick?shelf=trade-fiction">trade-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer776658453" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14067071521782102643">Does it sometime seem to you as though Indian novelists are muscling into the ranks of top English-language writers, making their way onto the best-seller lists and snapping up a disproportionate share of the literary awards? No? Think of Anita Desai, Arundhati Roy, Amitav Ghosh, Vikram Chanda, Kiran Desai, Aravind Adiga, and don’t forget Salman Rushdie. And that’s just those who come to mind without effort.<br/><br/>Within this pantheon of literary overachievers, the Indian-American writer Jhumpa Lahiri </span>
  <span id="freeText14067071521782102643" style="display:none">Does it sometime seem to you as though Indian novelists are muscling into the ranks of top English-language writers, making their way onto the best-seller lists and snapping up a disproportionate share of the literary awards? No? Think of Anita Desai, Arundhati Roy, Amitav Ghosh, Vikram Chanda, Kiran Desai, Aravind Adiga, and don’t forget Salman Rushdie. And that’s just those who come to mind without effort.<br /><br />Within this pantheon of literary overachievers, the Indian-American writer Jhumpa Lahiri fits comfortably. She won the Pulitzer Prize for her first collection of short stories, Interpreter of Maladies. The Lowland, the second of Lahiri’s novels, was shortlisted for the National Book Award this year. (Her first novel wasn’t overlooked, either, gaining bestseller status and receiving rave reviews like pretty much everything else she’s written.)<br /><br />On one level, The Lowland reflects two sides of its author’s persona — her Bengali heritage (though she was born in the UK) and her upbringing in Rhode Island. The novel’s action is divided between the middle-class precincts of Kolkata (formerly Calcutta) and the academic world of Rhode Island. The plot revolves around two momentous incidents, one taking place in Kolkata in 1971, the other in Rhode Island twelve years later.<br /><br />In another sense, the novel is a tale of two Bengali brothers, preternaturally close in their youth, whose passions take them in divergent directions. Subhash, older by fifteen months, emerges as the protagonist, pursuing a vocation for marine biology through graduate studies, laboratory work, and teaching in Rhode Island. The more adventurous Udayan becomes caught up in the violent revolutionary politics of the Naxalite Rebellion, a Maoist insurgency that continues to rage throughout much of northeastern and southern India.<br /><br />Lahiri’s sense of history and its consequences is as insightful as her grasp of the human heart. She weaves her tale with a sure hand around the threads of the two brother’s intersecting lives, moving swiftly back and forth from one continent to the other and ranging across the seven decades from Subhash’s birth in 1943 to the present day. It’s difficult to read this story without succumbing to the illusion that Lahiri’s vividly drawn characters are real. The Lowland is a sad story of love and loss, but a hopeful one nonetheless. Lahiri never lets us get lost in despair, finding hope and possibility in birth, renewal, and forgiveness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14067071521782102643" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_676708518" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/676708518" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/676708518?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=contemp-novel">contemp-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=immigrants">immigrants</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=love">love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=life-stages">life-stages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=lgtb">lgtb</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2020-read">2020-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer676708518" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14114496962957040129">I should feel more attached to the characters than I do. <br/><br/>The story is about two Indian brothers born fifteen months apart, the first in 1943 and the second in 1945. Despite their differences in personality, they are close, very close. The story continues more than a decade into the 21st century. They are born in Calcutta. The book concerns sibling relationships, what binds them and what divides them. One brother knows his goals and goes after them. Some might call this self-centered. The other h</span>
  <span id="freeText14114496962957040129" style="display:none">I should feel more attached to the characters than I do. <br /><br />The story is about two Indian brothers born fifteen months apart, the first in 1943 and the second in 1945. Despite their differences in personality, they are close, very close. The story continues more than a decade into the 21st century. They are born in Calcutta. The book concerns sibling relationships, what binds them and what divides them. One brother knows his goals and goes after them. Some might call this self-centered. The other has goals too but is more flexible in how to achieve them and more conscious of how his actions influence others. Their choices have repercussions on each other, on who each one will marry and on other members of the family--for generations to come. <br /><br />The backdrop is set by the historical events unfolding in India and the US during the brothers’ lives. One brother stays in India. One moves to America. In India, there arises the Naxalite Movement and then the Partition. In the US we see the Vietnam War and growth of flower power in the 1960s and early 1970s. We observe how this affects life on school campuses. These events are the backdrop, the central theme is however the relationships that arise. <br /><br />The author is known for writing about immigrants, and she does that here too. Each immigrant has a different story to tell. Their stories do not end with their own lives. The lives of their children and grandchildren are affected too. Immigration is an important component of the story told here. <br /><br />One woman comes to affect the lives of the two brothers. Her actions are central to the story told. Her behavior is, in my view, not properly explored. I want more of her thoughts. If I am to accept what she does, I need to understand how she is thinking. I can think through what she does, logically, in my own head, but to really understand her I need to have access to <u>her</u> thoughts. I have a very hard time accepting the manner in which she <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[abandons her daughter <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Could the schism be at least partially a result of differences in personal chemistry? Nothing is said of this. The book is over, and I am still looking for an answer. This I find unsatisfactory!<br /><br />The story uses flashbacks. As events occur readers are not given full information. Flashbacks later fill in the gaps. I do not like this. To fully understand a character’s motivation, I need as much information as possible as events unfold, not later! The flashbacks add clarity, but the information provided comes at the wrong point. The telling becomes disjointed. The flashbacks sum up, explain, what has happened earlier. Consequently, earlier events are told rather than shown. In them, dialogue is often lacking. Dialogue pulls a reader into a story, and so this we miss. The flashbacks do not improve the telling of the story. <br /><br />The story is too long and drawn out, partially, as a result of the flashbacks. <br /><br />There is a coldness, a reserve, a lack of spontaneity in the characters. This puts up a border between me and them. None, other than <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the younger brother, who dies early in the story <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, feel alive to me. <br /><br />There is no humor whatsoever. There is a restraint, a lack of vivacity, a depressing tone in the telling that I find draining. <br /><br />I cannot give the book more stars. The topics covered have potential. The potential I find unfulfilled. <br /><br />Suni Malhotra narrates the audiobook very well. His tone fits the mood of the characters and the story. It is not the narrator’s fault that characters do not come alive. His reading mirrors the author’s lines. Four stars for the audio narration.<br /><br />*****************<br /><br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17262100.The_Lowland" title="The Lowland by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lowland</a> 3 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33917.The_Namesake" title="The Namesake by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">The Namesake</a> 3 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5439.Interpreter_of_Maladies" title="Interpreter of Maladies by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">Interpreter of Maladies</a> 2 star</span>
  <a data-text-id="14114496962957040129" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/732320982?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shaunak Bhattacharya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shaunak Bhattacharya" href="/user/show/5216408-shaunak-bhattacharya">Shaunak Bhattacharya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer732320982" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer1284410176118783526">Very rarely after rating a book on Goodreads i have clicked "write a review"....very often....this is one such time....<br/><br/>The first line onwards this book was just a magic recreated with a delightful and mesmerising penmanship by Jhumpa Lahiri.... her previous books were good or even brilliant....but the way her storytelling has matured is simply a treat to the eyes and to the mind....<br/><br/>there is no relief in the storyline....it strangles you with one tragedy after the other which reminds you of the w</span>
  <span id="freeText1284410176118783526" style="display:none">Very rarely after rating a book on Goodreads i have clicked "write a review"....very often....this is one such time....<br /><br />The first line onwards this book was just a magic recreated with a delightful and mesmerising penmanship by Jhumpa Lahiri.... her previous books were good or even brilliant....but the way her storytelling has matured is simply a treat to the eyes and to the mind....<br /><br />there is no relief in the storyline....it strangles you with one tragedy after the other which reminds you of the works of Khaled Hosseini....of many incomplete affairs....she plays with the relations...breaks them....shapes them....recreates them....and she does that with sheer command over the language....<br /><br />there may not be enough lines to become memorable....but its the story and the paths it takes over six decades and over two halves of the world that makes this story a wonderful experience...<br /><br />may be one of the best experiences in a long time...<br /><br />and the characters...the building up of the characters...the vulnerability of each and every one of the characters adds to the tragedy of the book...yet...the way those vulnerability gives rise to the latent powers within them...makes this a winner...<br /><br />spanning over the Naxal period, the developments of the world across six decades....this story is a complete story of Love,Fraternity,Heartbreaks,Affection,Indifference....but mostly it stands for Rising from the ashes,burning again and crumbling down, and again resurrecting....<br /><br />thank you Jhumpa Lahiri....for keeping many relations incomplete....yet completing them in a very subtle way and for touching the heart in this Hosseinic manner...wish you all the success for your future...<br /><br />this story will be remembered....for days and years to come....</span>
  <a data-text-id="1284410176118783526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('HWp1Kyaofs+iP1AV2JbD3OcVqUpX40XoSzZJmqgFbhIvUH0MCDCCwN98AJ76UaVjGgXaXE1jUjHNPe/BIEgUIg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('y7T/Dlf9fb23xK8ous8bA9T3KNWdDLcWSiJnZyEoJXz5jvcpeWWBssqH/6OYCH28Kedbw4eMoM/MKcE8qWVfTA==')})
  }

He will witness the story of the first nation to defeat Genghis Khan’s army. He will walk alongside the revolutionary whose love for his wife was so strong that even the mighty British Empire could not break it. But the true message Avinash will realize is that the greatest gift Man has is their mind. And once the mind is unlocked, all the answers to Man’s problems will be right before their eyes.\n\nWhat Readers are Saying:\n&quot;I have been reading books by and about South Asians this year, and this one was a light, inspirational one. Poisonous rivalries between sisters-in-law, destructive secrets, and the implosion of the family business threaten to unravel bonds of kinship as social unrest brews in greater Indian society. This is a moment of turbulence, of inevitable and unstoppable change: the chasm between the generations, and between those who have and those who have not, has never been wider. The eldest grandchild, Supratik, compelled by his idealism, becomes dangerously involved in extremist political activism—an action that further catalyzes the decay of the Ghosh home.\n\n\nAmbitious, rich, and compassionate, The Lives of Others anatomizes the soul of a nation as it unfolds a family history, at the same time as it questions the nature of political action and the limits of empathy. It is a novel of unflinching power and emotional force.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"5816901047415406175\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3670.Jhumpa_Lahiri">About Jhumpa Lahiri</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3670.Jhumpa_Lahiri"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1378932972p3/3670.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/3670.Jhumpa_Lahiri">Jhumpa Lahiri

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3511925778202256998">Nilanjana Sudeshna "Jhumpa" Lahiri was born in London and brought up in South Kingstown, Rhode Island. Brought up in America by a mother who wanted to raise her children to be Indian, she learned about her Bengali heritage from an early age. <br/><br/>Lahiri graduated from South Kingstown High School and later received her B.A. in English literature from Barnard College in 1989. She then received multiple d</span>
  <span id="freeText3511925778202256998" style="display:none">Nilanjana Sudeshna "Jhumpa" Lahiri was born in London and brought up in South Kingstown, Rhode Island. Brought up in America by a mother who wanted to raise her children to be Indian, she learned about her Bengali heritage from an early age. <br /><br />Lahiri graduated from South Kingstown High School and later received her B.A. in English literature from Barnard College in 1989. She then received multiple degrees from Boston University: an M.A. in English, an M.A. in Creative Writing, an M.A. in Comparative Literature and a Ph.D. in Renaissance Studies. She took up a fellowship at Provincetown's Fine Arts Work Center, which lasted for the next two years (1997-1998).<br /><br />In 2001, she married Alberto Vourvoulias-Bush, a journalist who was then Deputy Editor of <i>TIME Latin America</i> Lahiri currently lives in Brooklyn with her husband and two children. She has been a Vice President of the PEN American Center since 2005.<br /><br />Lahiri taught creative writing at Boston University and the Rhode Island School of Design. Much of her short fiction concerns the lives of Indian-Americans, particularly Bengalis.<br /><br />She received the following awards, among others:<br />1999 - PEN/Hemingway Award (Best Fiction Debut of the Year) for <i>Interpreter of Maladies</i>;<br />2000 - The New Yorker's Best Debut of the Year for <i>Interpreter of Maladies</i>;<br />2000 - Pulitzer Prize for Fiction for her debut <i>Interpreter of Maladies</i> </span>
  <a data-text-id="3511925778202256998" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/23858897-the-lowland">Quotes from The Lowland</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Isolation offered its own form of companionship: the reliable silence of her rooms, the steadfast tranquility of the evenings.
